###begin article-title 0
###xml 18 22 18 22 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 75 80 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Positive roles of SAS2 in DNA replication and transcriptional silencing in yeast
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
###xml 0 16 0 16 <!--CREATIVE COMMONS-->
CREATIVE COMMONSThis is an Open Access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution Non-Commercial License () which permits unrestricted non-commercial use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited.
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 297 301 297 301 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 312 321 312 317 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 382 388 378 384 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 393 399 389 395 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc5-1</italic>
###xml 411 420 407 412 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 425 431 417 423 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 560 564 552 556 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 652 661 644 649 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 666 672 654 660 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc5-1</italic>
###xml 733 736 721 724 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 774 783 762 767 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 890 894 874 878 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 965 969 949 953 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 272 277 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Sas2p is a histone acetyltransferase implicated in the regulation of transcriptional silencing, and ORC is the six-subunit origin recognition complex involved in the initiation of DNA replication and the establishment of transcriptionally silent chromatin by silencers in yeast. We show here that SAS2 deletion (sas2Delta) exacerbates the temperature sensitivity of the ORC mutants orc2-1 and orc5-1. Moreover, sas2Delta and orc2-1 have a synthetic effect on cell cycle progression through S phase and initiation of DNA replication. These results suggest that SAS2 plays a positive role in DNA replication and cell cycle progression. We also show that sas2Delta and orc5-1 have a synthetic effect on transcriptional silencing at the HMR locus. Moreover, we demonstrate that sas2Delta reduces the silencing activities of silencers regardless of their locations and contexts, indicating that SAS2 plays a positive role in silencer function. In addition, we show that SAS2 is required for maintaining the structure of transcriptionally silent chromatin.
###end p 2
###begin title 3
INTRODUCTION
###end title 3
###begin p 4
###xml 500 501 500 501 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B1">1</xref>
###xml 879 880 879 880 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B2">2</xref>
###xml 881 882 881 882 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 898 922 898 922 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1156 1157 1156 1157 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B2">2</xref>
###xml 1292 1293 1292 1293 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B3">3</xref>
###xml 898 922 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
Eukaryotic DNA is packed into chromatin through the formation of nucleosomes. Chromatin can be roughly divided into condensed heterochromatin and decondensed euchromatin based on its cytological and molecular properties. Chromatin plays a pivotal role in the regulation of DNA-dependent processes including gene expression, DNA replication and DNA repair. Chromatin is subject to various modifications that differentially affect its compaction and accessibility to DNA/chromatin-interacting factors (1). Acetylation of lysine residues of histones may alter nucleosomal conformation and/or chromatin compaction. Histone acetylation is usually associated with euchromatin, whereas histone hypoacetylation is a hallmark of heterochromatin. Histone acetylation is carried out by histone acetyltransferases (HATs) that fall into distinct families with specific substrate preferences (2,3). For example, Saccharomyces cerevisiae Sas2p belongs to the MYST (MOZ, Ybf2/Sas3, Sas2 and Tip60) HAT family that preferentially acetylates histones H4 and H2A. A native HAT usually functions as part of a multi-subunit complex that helps direct the HAT to proper targets (2). Sas2p is the catalytic subunit of the SAS complex consisting of Sas2p, Sas4p and Sas5p that specifically acetylates histone H4-K16 (3).
###end p 4
###begin p 5
###xml 3 16 3 16 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 131 132 131 132 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B4">4</xref>
###xml 497 498 497 498 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B4">4</xref>
###xml 747 748 747 748 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B5">5</xref>
###xml 878 879 878 879 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B6">6</xref>
###xml 3 16 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
In S. cerevisiae, DNA replication is initiated at defined origins named autonomous replicating sequences (ARSs) across the genome (4). The origin recognition complex (ORC) plays a key role in replication initiation. ORC is composed of six subunits (Orc1p-Orc6p), all of which are required for cell survival. ORC associates with replication origins throughout the cell cycle, but its main function is to nucleate an initiation competent pre-replication complex (pre-RC) at origins in the G1 phase (4). Defects in pre-RC formation caused by ORC mutations may result in incomplete DNA replication and increased chances of replication fork stalling/breakage that triggers DNA damage and spindle assembly checkpoint pathways leading to mitotic arrest (5). There is also evidence that ORC participates in sister-chromatid cohesion, which is distinct from its role in DNA replication (6).
###end p 5
###begin p 6
###xml 189 190 189 190 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B7">7</xref>
###xml 191 192 191 192 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B8">8</xref>
###xml 277 278 277 278 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 458 459 458 459 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B9">9</xref>
###xml 460 462 460 462 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B10">10</xref>
###xml 598 600 598 600 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B11">11</xref>
###xml 685 689 685 689 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HAT1</italic>
###xml 733 736 733 736 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 746 748 746 748 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B12">12</xref>
###xml 983 985 983 985 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B13">13</xref>
###xml 986 988 986 988 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B14">14</xref>
The timing and efficiency of the firing of replication origins appear to depend on their genomic locations and contexts. There is evidence that chromatin structure affects origin activity (7,8). Histone acetylation surrounding an origin directly influences its time of firing (9). Removal of the histone deacetylase (HDAC) Rpd3p causes DNA replication to occur early in S-phase, and targeting the HAT Gcn5p to a late firing origin causes it to fire earlier (9,10). There is also evidence that the HDAC Sir2p involved in transcriptional silencing negatively regulates initiation of DNA replication (11). The HAT Hat1p was recently shown to physically interact with ORC, and deletion of HAT1 exacerbated the temperature sensitivity of orc mutants (12). These studies suggest that HATs play positive roles, and HDACs play negative roles in the initiation of DNA replication. However, interestingly, there is also evidence suggesting that the HAT Sas2p negatively regulate ORC function (13,14).
###end p 6
###begin p 7
###xml 111 113 111 113 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B15">15</xref>
###xml 153 156 153 156 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 161 164 161 164 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 185 188 185 188 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">cis</italic>
###xml 196 197 196 197 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E</italic>
###xml 202 203 202 203 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">I</italic>
###xml 235 237 235 237 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B15">15</xref>
###xml 623 625 623 625 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B15">15</xref>
###xml 626 628 626 628 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B16">16</xref>
###xml 46 51 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
ORC also plays a role in the establishment of yeast silent chromatin that is akin to metazoan heterochromatin (15). Formation of silent chromatin at the HML and HMR loci is promoted by cis-acting E and I silencers flanking these loci (15). Each silencer consists of recognition sites for ORC, Rap1p and/or Abf1p. The silencer-binding factors recruit the Sir complex composed of Sir2p, Sir3p and Sir4p through a direct interaction between ORC and Sir1p, which binds to Sir4p, and the binding of Rap1p to Sir3p or Sir4p. Sir2p is a HDAC that is responsible for characteristic hypoacetylation of histones in silent chromatin (15,16). The Sir complex recruited to a silencer is believed to deacetylate histones in adjacent nucleosomes. The newly deacetylated nucleosomes then bind additional Sir complexes. This is because the Sir complex self-interacts and preferentially binds hypoacetylated histones. Through repeated cycles of histone deacetylation and Sir complex recruitment, Sir complexes propagate along an array of nucleosomes.
###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 66 70 66 70 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 81 90 81 86 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 139 142 135 138 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 180 185 176 181 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR-E</italic>
###xml 209 212 205 208 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 230 239 226 231 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sir1&#916;</italic>
###xml 252 254 244 246 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B13">13</xref>
###xml 255 257 247 249 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 292 301 284 289 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 380 383 368 371 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 391 393 379 381 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B18">18</xref>
###xml 394 396 382 384 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
###xml 410 414 398 402 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 478 480 466 468 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HM</italic>
###xml 575 584 563 568 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 645 647 629 631 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 778 780 762 764 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 781 783 765 767 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
###xml 961 963 945 947 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B20">20</xref>
###xml 964 966 948 950 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B21">21</xref>
Silent chromatin is regulated by various factors including Sas2p. SAS2 deletion (sas2Delta) was originally found to suppress the defect in HMR silencing caused by mutations in the HMR-E silencer, but decrease HML silencing in the sir1Delta background (13,17). However, it was also found that sas2Delta decreased silencing of ectopic reporter genes inserted at an otherwise intact HMR locus (18,19). Therefore, SAS2 seems to have the ability to positively or negatively regulate HM silencing. What determines the mode of Sas2p action has not been resolved. On the other hand, sas2Delta has been consistently found to abolish telomeric silencing (17), and it is proposed that Sas2p contributes to telomeric silencing by helping retain Sir complexes in telomeric silent chromatin (20,21). This is based on the finding that acetylation of H4-K16 in subtelomeric euchromatic regions by Sas2p hinders ectopic spreading of Sir complex from telomeric silent chromatin (20,21).
###end p 8
###begin p 9
###xml 172 181 172 177 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 225 231 221 227 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 236 242 232 238 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc5-1</italic>
###xml 275 284 271 276 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 368 372 360 364 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 380 386 372 378 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 588 591 580 583 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 595 598 587 590 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
In this report, we examined functional relationships between Sas2p and ORC in DNA replication and transcriptional silencing in different genetic backgrounds. We found that sas2Delta exacerbated the temperature sensitivity of orc2-1 and orc5-1 mutants. We further showed that sas2Delta retarded cell cycle progression through S-phase and reduced the origin activity of ARS1 in the orc2-1 mutant. These results suggest that Sas2p plays a positive role in DNA replication. We also obtained evidence suggesting that Sas2p positively regulates silencer function regardless of whether it is at HML or HMR. Moreover, we showed that Sas2p played a role in maintaining transcriptionally silent chromatin structure.
###end p 9

MATERIALS AND METHODS
###end title 10

###xml 0 5 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
###xml 1117 1122 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
Yeast strains are listed in Table 1. Strains carrying the sas2Delta::kanMX allele were made by transforming their corresponding parents to geneticin-resistant with a PCR-generated fragment composed of kanMX bracketed by 5' and 3' flanking sequences of the SAS2 open reading frame (ORF). Strains carrying the sas2Delta1::TRP1 allele were made by transforming their parents with plasmid pJR1642 (13) digested with XhoI and BamHI. Plasmid pUC-HMR was made by inserting the genomic HindIII-HMR-Hind III sequence (289 227-294 210 of chromosome III) into pUC12. An XbaI-URA3-XbaI fragment was used to replace an XbaI fragment of pUC-HMR, making p206. Strains 36 and 37 were made by transforming strains 34 and 35, respectively, to Ura+ with plasmid pES17 (22) digested with BamHI and Hind III. Strains 38 and 39 were made by transforming strains 34 and 35, respectively, to Ura+ with EcoRI-digested plasmid pDM42 (23). Strains 40 and 42 were made by transforming strains 11 and 15, respectively, to Ura+ with HindIII-digested plasmid p206. The relevant genotypes of all strains were confirmed by Southern blotting. ###begin title 14
Fluorescence activated cell sorting
###end title 14
###begin p 15
###xml 103 106 97 100 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">600</sub>
###xml 264 266 240 242 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B24">24</xref>
Cells for fluorescence activated cell sorting (FACS) analysis were grown at 23degreesC to log phase (OD600 = approximately0.5) and divided into two aliquots that were grown for another 90 min at 23 and 30degreesC, respectively. Samples were prepared as described (24) and analyzed on a FACSCalibur (Becton, Dickinson and Company, Franklin Lakes, NJ, USA). Data acquisition was performed with the CellQuest software. Quantification of the percentages of cells in G1 phase (with C1 DNA content), S-phase and G2-M (with C2 DNA content) were performed using the MODFIT software program (Verity Software House).
###end p 15
###begin title 16
Two-dimensional gel electrophoresis
###end title 16
###begin p 17
###xml 221 222 209 210 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">9</sup>
###xml 293 295 281 283 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B25">25</xref>
###xml 380 382 368 370 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>
###xml 383 385 371 373 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B27">27</xref>
###xml 652 656 633 637 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 693 697 674 678 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 733 737 714 718 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 767 773 748 754 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS305</italic>
###xml 810 816 791 797 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS305</italic>
Cells used for isolating replication intermediates (RIs) were grown at 23degreesC to log phase and divided into two aliquots that were grown for another 90 min at 23 and 30degreesC, respectively. For each sample, 1.5 x 109 cells were collected and used for genomic DNA isolation as described (25). Genomic DNA was digested with NcoI and subjected to 2D gel analysis as described (26,27). The conditions for the first dimension were: 0.35% agarose, 1 x TBE buffer, 0.6 V/cm for 45 h, room temperature; and for the second dimension: 0.95% agarose, 0.3 mug/ml ethidium bromide, 2.8 V/cm for 20 h, 4degreesC. The Southern blot was first hybridized with an ARS1 probe to reveal RIs associated with ARS1. The blot was then stripped of the ARS1 probe and hybridized with an ARS305 probe to reveal RIs associated with ARS305.
###end p 17
###begin title 18
Chromatin mapping
###end title 18
###begin p 19
###xml 48 50 48 50 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B28">28</xref>
###xml 65 66 65 66 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">8</sup>
Chromatin mapping was carried out as described (28). About 2 x 108 permeabilized spheroplasts were treated with MNase at 120 or 160 U/ml at 37degreesC for 5 min. Purified genomic (naked) DNA from untreated cells was digested with MNase at 7.5 U/ml.
###end p 19
###begin title 20
Analysis of DNA topology
###end title 20
###begin p 21
###xml 207 212 207 212 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">GAL10</italic>
###xml 213 217 213 217 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">FLP1</italic>
###xml 35 40 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Cells were grown in YPR medium (1% yeast extract, 2% bacto-peptone and 2% raffinose) to log phase. Galactose (2%) was added to the culture, which was further incubated for 2.5 h to induce the expression of PGAL10-FLP1. Nucleic acids were isolated using the glass bead method and fractionated on an agarose gel supplemented with 13 mug/ml chloroquine. DNA circles were detected by Southern blotting.
###end p 21

###xml 1438 2323 1384 2229 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="25">Synthetic interactions between <italic>sas2&#916;</italic> and <italic>orc</italic> mutations. ###xml 1438 2323 1384 2229 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="25">Synthetic interactions between <italic>sas2&#916;</italic> and <italic>orc</italic> mutations. ###xml 2323 2323 2229 2229 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkn465f1"/>
###xml 1429 2323 1375 2229 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F1" position="float"><label>Figure 1.</label><caption><p textid="25">Synthetic interactions between <italic>sas2&#916;</italic> and <italic>orc</italic> mutations. (<bold>A</bold>) Synthetic interactions between <italic>sas2&#916;</italic> and <italic>orc</italic> mutations in the <italic>MATa</italic> background. Cells of strains 1&#8211;10 were grown to log phase in synthetic complete (SC) liquid medium. Serial 10-fold dilutions were spotted on four SC plates and incubated for 3 days at 23, 30, 34 and 37&#176;C, respectively. Strains 1, 3, 9 and 10 were also grown at 26&#176;C. Cells were also spotted on SC plates supplemented with 50 mM HU and incubated at 23&#176;C for 3 days. Two independent clones of each strain were examined. (<bold>B</bold>) Synthetic interactions between <italic>sas2&#916;</italic> and <italic>orc</italic> mutations in the <italic>MAT&#945;</italic> background. Cells of strains 11&#8211;20 were grown to log phase and serial 10-fold dilutions were spotted on SC plates and incubated for 3 days at 23, 26, 30 and 34&#176;C, respectively. Two independent clones of each strain were examined.</p></caption><graphic xlink:href="gkn465f1"/></fig>
All six subunits of ORC, Orc1p-Orc6p, are essential for cell viability, but temperature sensitive mutations of the ORC genes such as orc2-1 and orc5-1 have been described (4,29,30). It was reported previously that sas2Delta partially suppressed the temperature-sensitivity of the orc2-1 mutant (13). In an attempt to further examine functional interactions between the SAS2 and ORC genes, we made orc2-1 sas2Delta::kanMX and orc5-1 sas2Delta::kanMX double mutants in the W303-1A (MATa) background. We were surprised to find that our orc2-1 sas2Delta double mutant grew less robustly than the orc2-1 and sas2Delta single mutants at 30degreesC, a semi-permissive temperature for orc2-1 (Figure 1A, compare 4 to 3). In line with this finding, our orc5-1 sas2Delta double mutant also showed reduced growth compare to the orc5-1 single mutant at the semi-permissive temperature of 34degreesC (Figure 1A, compare 7 to 6). These results suggest that sas2Delta has a synthetic effect on cell growth with orc2-1 or orc5-1. Since Sas2p is a subunit of the SAS complex that also contains Sas4p and Sas5p, we asked whether SAS4 also genetically interacts with the ORC genes. We found that similar to sas2Delta, sas4Delta exacerbated the growth defect of orc2-1 at semi-permissive temperatures (Figure 1A, compare 10 to 3 at 26 and 30degreesC). These results suggest that the SAS complex performs an overlapping role with ORC in cell growth. Figure 1.Synthetic interactions between sas2Delta and orc mutations. (A) Synthetic interactions between sas2Delta and orc mutations in the MATa background. Cells of strains 1-10 were grown to log phase in synthetic complete (SC) liquid medium. Serial 10-fold dilutions were spotted on four SC plates and incubated for 3 days at 23, 30, 34 and 37degreesC, respectively. Strains 1, 3, 9 and 10 were also grown at 26degreesC. Cells were also spotted on SC plates supplemented with 50 mM HU and incubated at 23degreesC for 3 days. Two independent clones of each strain were examined. (B) Synthetic interactions between sas2Delta and orc mutations in the MATalpha background. Cells of strains 11-20 were grown to log phase and serial 10-fold dilutions were spotted on SC plates and incubated for 3 days at 23, 26, 30 and 34degreesC, respectively. Two independent clones of each strain were examined.
###end p 24
###begin p 25
###xml 31 40 31 36 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 45 48 41 44 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 61 62 57 58 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</bold>
###xml 95 104 91 96 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 109 112 101 104 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 130 134 122 126 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MATa</italic>
###xml 572 573 546 547 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</bold>
###xml 606 615 580 585 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 620 623 590 593 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 641 649 611 615 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAT&#945;</italic>
Synthetic interactions between sas2Delta and orc mutations. (A) Synthetic interactions between sas2Delta and orc mutations in the MATa background. Cells of strains 1-10 were grown to log phase in synthetic complete (SC) liquid medium. Serial 10-fold dilutions were spotted on four SC plates and incubated for 3 days at 23, 30, 34 and 37degreesC, respectively. Strains 1, 3, 9 and 10 were also grown at 26degreesC. Cells were also spotted on SC plates supplemented with 50 mM HU and incubated at 23degreesC for 3 days. Two independent clones of each strain were examined. (B) Synthetic interactions between sas2Delta and orc mutations in the MATalpha background. Cells of strains 11-20 were grown to log phase and serial 10-fold dilutions were spotted on SC plates and incubated for 3 days at 23, 26, 30 and 34degreesC, respectively. Two independent clones of each strain were examined.
###end p 25
###begin p 26
###xml 138 141 138 141 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 154 163 154 159 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 270 279 266 271 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 331 335 323 327 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 368 373 360 365 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">kanMX</italic>
###xml 431 435 423 427 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 469 473 461 465 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">TRP1</italic>
###xml 506 510 498 502 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 528 544 520 532 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 546 548 534 536 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B13">13</xref>
###xml 549 551 537 539 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B14">14</xref>
###xml 582 598 570 582 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 757 773 741 753 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 788 804 768 780 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;::kanMX</italic>
###xml 829 835 805 811 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 839 845 815 821 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc5-1</italic>
###xml 855 878 831 850 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1 sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 883 906 855 874 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc5-1 sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 1035 1058 1003 1022 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1 sas2&#916;::kanMX</italic>
###xml 1063 1086 1027 1046 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc5-1 sas2&#916;::kanMX</italic>
###xml 1110 1118 1070 1078 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 1177 1193 1137 1149 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;::kanMX</italic>
###xml 1198 1214 1154 1166 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 1247 1253 1199 1205 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 1257 1263 1209 1215 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">cor5-1</italic>
###xml 1290 1294 1242 1246 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
As we considered the cause of the discrepancy between our results and previously published results regarding genetic interactions between orc mutants and sas2Delta, we noted that the genetic background of our strains was different from that of the previous strains. Our sas2Delta strains differed from published ones in the way of SAS2 disruption. In our strains, the kanMX cassette was used to precisely replace the entire ORF of SAS2, whereas in the previous strains TRP1 was used to replace most of the SAS2 ORF resulting in sas2Delta1::TRP1 (13,14). We analyzed the sequence of sas2Delta1::TRP1 and found it contained a new ORF encoding a short peptide composed of the N-terminal four amino acids of Sas2p followed by 20 other residues. To test whether sas2Delta1::TRP1 differed from sas2Delta::kanMX in its interaction with orc2-1 or orc5-1, we made orc2-1 sas2Delta1::TRP1 and orc5-1 sas2Delta1::TRP1 double mutants in the W303-1A background. The growth phenotypes of these double mutants at various temperatures were similar to orc2-1 sas2Delta::kanMX and orc5-1 sas2Delta::kanMX strains, respectively (Figure 1A, compare 5 to 4 and 3, and 8 to 7 and 6). Therefore, the sas2Delta::kanMX and sas2Delta1::TRP1 alleles interact similarly with orc2-1 or cor5-1, ruling out the method of SAS2 disruption as the cause of the discrepancy between our data and previously published results.
###end p 26
###begin p 27
###xml 4 7 4 7 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 70 78 70 74 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAT&#945;</italic>
###xml 100 104 96 100 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MATa</italic>
###xml 184 187 180 183 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 200 209 196 201 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 221 244 213 232 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1 sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 249 272 237 256 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc5-1 sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 356 359 340 343 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 466 469 450 453 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 486 494 470 478 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 595 603 579 587 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 654 657 638 641 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 689 697 673 677 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAT&#945;</italic>
###xml 721 725 701 705 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MATa</italic>
###xml 744 759 724 735 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAT&#945; orc2-1</italic>
###xml 815 826 785 796 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MATa orc2-1</italic>
###xml 855 863 825 833 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
The orc mutants used in previous studies were derivatives of W303-1B (MATalpha) instead of W303-1A (MATa). We therefore examined if the cell type affected genetic interactions between orc mutants and sas2Delta. We remade orc2-1 sas2Delta1::TRP1 and orc5-1 sas2Delta1::TRP1 double mutants in the W303-1B background and compared their growth phenotypes with orc single mutants. The double mutants exhibited more severe temperature sensitivity than their corresponding orc single mutants (Figure 1B, compare 14 to 13, and 16 to 15), which was similar to our results concerning W303-1A derivatives (Figure 1A). We noticed that the temperature sensitivity of orc mutants was more severe in the MATalpha background than in the MATa background as the MATalpha orc2-1 strain was not able to grow at 30degreesC, whereas the MATa orc2-1 strain was (albeit poorly) (Figure 1, compare 13 to 3). Possible causes of this phenomenon are noted later in the Discussion section.
###end p 27
###begin p 28
###xml 101 104 101 104 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 128 133 128 133 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR-E</italic>
###xml 163 168 163 168 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR-I</italic>
###xml 179 191 179 187 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR-SS&#916;I</italic>
###xml 194 196 190 192 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B13">13</xref>
###xml 242 245 238 241 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 275 298 271 290 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1 sas2&#916;1::TRP1</italic>
###xml 306 327 298 311 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAT&#945; HMR-SS&#916;I</italic>
###xml 404 410 388 394 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 426 434 410 418 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
Another difference between our strains and previous strains was that the latter contained a modified HMR locus with a synthetic HMR-E silencer and deletion of the HMR-I silencer (HMR-SSDeltaI) (13), whereas all our strains were wild-type for HMR. We remade the double mutant orc2-1 sas2Delta1::TRP1 in the MATalpha HMR-SSDeltaI background and found that it still exhibited growth defects compared to the orc2-1 single mutant (Figure 1B, compare 20 to 19).
###end p 28
###begin p 29
###xml 32 41 32 37 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 85 88 81 84 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc</italic>
###xml 146 152 142 148 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 258 266 254 262 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figuer 1</xref>
###xml 333 342 329 334 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 352 358 344 350 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 398 406 390 398 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 497 501 489 493 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
The above data demonstrate that sas2Delta exacerbates the temperature sensitivity of orc mutants. In addition to its temperature sensitivity, the orc2-1 mutant is sensitive to hydroxyurea (HU), which induces replicative DNA damage at permissive temperature (Figuer 1A, compare 3 to 1 on HU-containing medium; ref. 31). We found that sas2Delta rendered orc2-1 cells moderately more sensitive to HU (Figure 1A, compare 4 and 5 to 3 on HU-containing medium). Taken together, our results suggest that SAS2 plays a positive role in ORC-mediated functions.
###end p 29
###begin title 30
###xml 20 29 20 25 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 34 40 30 36 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
Synthetic effect of sas2Delta and orc2-1 on cell cycle progression through S-phase
###end title 30
###begin p 31

ORC is involved in the initiation of DNA replication, and defects in ORC function interrupt cell cycle progression (30,32,33). To further examine the role of Sas2p in ORC-mediated functions, we tested whether sas2Delta affected cell cycle progression of ORC2 and orc2-1 cells using FACS (fluorescence-activated cell sorting) analysis. Exponentially growing asynchronous cultures of two independent clones of each strain were subjected to FACS analysis and the results are shown in Figure 2A. The percentages of cells in G1 phase (with C1 DNA content), S-phase, and G2-M (with C2 DNA content) were measured for each culture and shown in Figure 2B. Figure 2.Synthetic effect of sas2Delta and orc2-1 on cell cycle progression. (A) FACS analysis. Cells were grown to log phase and subjected to FACS analysis. Two independent clones of each strain grown at 23 and 30degreesC, respectively, were examined. The x-axis represents DNA content and the y-axis the number of cells in the culture. Data for strains 1-4 at 23degreesC are shown in panels a-d, respectively, and data for strains 1-4 at 30degreesC are shown in panels e-h, respectively. (B) Quantification of the proportions of G1, S and G2-M cells. The percentages of G1, S and G2-M cells for each strain are the averages of data for the two independent clones examined by FACS in (A).

###begin p 32
###xml 20 29 20 25 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>

###xml 248 249 238 239 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">x</italic>
###xml 286 287 276 277 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">y</italic>
###xml 482 483 460 461 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">B</bold>
Synthetic effect of sas2Delta and orc2-1 on cell cycle progression. (A) FACS analysis. Cells were grown to log phase and subjected to FACS analysis. Two independent clones of each strain grown at 23 and 30degreesC, respectively, were examined. The x-axis represents DNA content and the y-axis the number of cells in the culture. Data for strains 1-4 at 23degreesC are shown in panels a-d, respectively, and data for strains 1-4 at 30degreesC are shown in panels e-h, respectively. (B) Quantification of the proportions of G1, S and G2-M cells. The percentages of G1, S and G2-M cells for each strain are the averages of data for the two independent clones examined by FACS in (A).

At 23degreesC, the wild-type culture has about 21% G1, 38% S and 41% G2-M cells (Figure 2A), which was not significantly affected by sas2Delta (Figure 2, compare b to a). The orc2-1 reduced the proportion of cells in G1 (Figure 2, compare c to a), which is consistent with previous results showing that temperature-sensitive mutations in ORC1, ORC2 and ORC5 all reduce the proportion of G1 cells (33). The sas2Delta had no effect on the proportions of G1, S and G2-M cells in the orc2-1 culture (Figure 2, compare d to c). After being incubated at the semi-permissive temperature of 30degreesC for 90 min, SAS2 and sas2Delta cells were roughly equally divided among the G1, S and G2-M phases (Figure 2B, E and F). As for the orc2-1 mutant, the proportion of G1 cells decreased, whereas that of S or G2-M cells increased at 30degreesC (Figure 2, compare g to c), which suggests that compromising Orc2p function induces a delay of progression through S and/or G2-M phases. Compared to orc2-1 cells, a strikingly larger percentage of orc2-1 sas2Delta cells were in S-phase at 30degreesC than at 23degreesC (Figure 3, compare h to g), indicating that sas2Delta delayed or arrested progression of orc2-1 cells through S-phase. Figure 3.Synthetic effect of sas2Delta and orc2-1 on the initiation of DNA replication. (A) Scheme of replication intermediates fractionated by 2D gel electrophoresis. The bubble arc, small- and large-fork arcs are indicated. (B) Schematics of the genomic NcoI fragment of chromosome IV containing ARS1 and the NcoI fragment of chromosome III containing ARS305. The ACS (ARS consensus sequence), B1-B4 and DUE sequence in the two ARSs are shown. Note that ACS is the recognition site of ORC, and B3 is an Abf1p-binding site. (C) 2D gel analysis of replication intermediates. Data for ARS1 in strains 1-4 at 23degreesC are shown in panels a-d, respectively, and data for ARS1 in strains 1-4 at 30degreesC are shown in panels e-h, respectively. Data for ARS305 in strains 3 and 4 at 23degreesC are shown in panels c' and d', respectively. Data for ARS305 in strains 3 and 4 at 30degreesC are shown in g' and h', respectively. See text for descriptions.
###end p 33

###begin title 35
###xml 0 4 0 4 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
SAS2 plays a positive role in ORC-dependent initiation of DNA replication
###end title 35
###begin p 36
###xml 31 35 31 35 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 72 78 72 78 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 252 261 252 257 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 410 414 406 410 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 479 485 475 481 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 490 499 486 491 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 680 682 672 674 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B26">26</xref>
###xml 865 873 857 865 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
The above results suggest that SAS2 is important for the passage of the orc2-1 mutant through S-phase. Sas2p may regulate replication initiation that is ORC-dependent, and/or replication elongation that is ORC-independent. We directly examined whether sas2Delta affected initiation of DNA replication. A neutral-neutral 2D gel electrophoresis technique was used to examine the abundance of RIs associated with ARS1, an early-firing origin of replication, in wild-type as well as orc2-1 and sas2Delta single and double mutants. This type of 2D gel separates replication bubbles, forks and linear DNA based on the size and shape of the DNA fragment containing a replication origin (26). A fragment containing an active replication origin in the middle would yield a bubble arc, whereas a fragment being replicated by passing replication forks would yield a fork arc (Figure 3A).
###end p 36
###begin p 37
###xml 0 4 0 4 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 28 37 28 33 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 39 45 35 41 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 51 67 47 59 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916; orc2-1</italic>
###xml 416 420 390 394 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 508 516 482 490 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 544 548 518 522 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 591 595 565 569 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 747 755 721 729 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
ARS1 activity in wild-type, sas2Delta, orc2-1, and sas2Delta orc2-1 strains was examined. For each strain, cells were grown to log phase at 23degreesC and divided into two halves that were grown for another 90 min, one at 23degreesC and the other at 30degreesC, before being harvested. Genomic DNA was isolated from each sample and digested with NcoI, and subjected to 2D gel analysis followed by Southern blotting. ARS1 is located in a 4.7 kb NcoI fragment of chromosome IV (coordinates 4 60 989-4 64 386) (Figure 3B). Because the position of ARS1 is asymmetric, replication initiated from ARS1 will initially yield bubbles and will then produce large forks when one of the two replication forks reaches one end of the fragment before the other (Figure 3A).
###end p 37

###xml 830 834 804 808 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 850 856 824 830 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 902 908 870 876 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 995 1001 963 969 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 1003 1011 971 979 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 1078 1094 1046 1058 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1 sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 1124 1132 1082 1090 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 1234 1238 1192 1196 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 1245 1251 1203 1209 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
In the wild-type strain at either 23 or 30degreesC, the majority of the RIs were bubbles and large forks, which is consistent with the notion that ARS1 is an active origin (Figure 3C, a and e). The presence of some small forks indicated that the ARS1 did not fire in a small fraction of the cells in the culture (Figure 3C, a and e; ref. 34). The sas2Delta alone had little or no effect on ARS1's origin activity at either 23 or 30degreesC (Figure 3C, compare b to a, and f to e). The orc2-1 mutation reduced ARS1 activity at 23degreesC as evidenced by a decrease in the relative intensity of the bubble arc compared to the fork arc (Figure 3C, compare c to a). The sas2Delta further reduced the relative intensity of the bubble arc in the orc2-1 mutant (Figure 3C, compare d to c), suggesting that SAS2 is required for efficient ARS1 function in an orc2-1 mutant. At 30degreesC, the bubble arc in the orc2-1 mutant was barely detectable, which is consistent with the temperature sensitivity of orc2-1 (Figure 3C, compare g to c). We were not able to detect a bubble arc in the orc2-1 sas2Delta double mutant at 30degreesC (Figure 3C, h). These results suggest that Sas2p plays a positive role in the initiation of DNA replication at ARS1 in an orc2-1 mutant.
###end p 38
###begin p 39
###xml 23 32 23 28 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 85 91 81 87 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 112 118 108 114 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS305</italic>
###xml 174 178 170 174 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 202 210 198 206 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 214 220 210 216 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS305</italic>
###xml 327 333 323 329 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 454 462 444 452 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 486 492 476 482 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS305</italic>
###xml 616 624 594 602 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 700 708 678 686 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 732 738 710 716 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS305</italic>
###xml 761 767 739 745 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 782 786 760 764 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 788 796 766 774 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 822 831 800 805 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 862 868 836 842 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS305</italic>
###xml 885 891 859 865 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 931 939 899 907 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 998 1002 966 970 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS1</italic>
###xml 1030 1036 998 1004 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ARS305</italic>
###xml 1061 1067 1029 1035 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">orc2-1</italic>
###xml 1072 1081 1040 1045 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
We next tested whether sas2Delta also affected the activities of other origins in an orc2-1 mutant. We examined ARS305, which is also an early firing origin but differs from ARS1 in sequence/structure (Figure 3B). ARS305 is asymmetrically located in a 5.1 kb NcoI fragment of chromosome III (coordinates 36 563-41 669). In the orc2-1 mutant, bubble and large-fork arcs were readily detectable, but little or no small-fork arc was observed at 23degreesC (Figure 3C, c'), suggesting that ARS305 was a very active origin. Raising the temperature from 23degreesC to 30degreesC increased the intensity of small fork arc (Figure 3C, compare g' to c'). However, the bubble arc was still readily detectable (Figure 3C, g'), indicating that ARS305 was less sensitive to orc2-1 mutation than ARS1 (Figure 3C, compare g' and g). The sas2Delta does not significantly affect ARS305 activity in the orc2-1 background at either 23 or 30degreesC (Figure 3C, compare d' to c', and h' to g'). Therefore, compared to ARS1, replication initiation at ARS305 seems less sensitive to orc2-1 and sas2Delta.
###end p 39
###begin title 40
###xml 0 4 0 4 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
SAS2 does not affect chromatin structure at replication origins
###end title 40
###begin p 41

ORC bound to a replication origin serves as a platform for the formation of the pre-RC. Meanwhile, it also directs the establishment of proper chromatin structure around the origin that is important for its function (8). Because Sas2p is a HAT that can acetylate histone H4 in chromatin, and plays a positive role in replication initiation at ARS1, we wondered whether it contributes to chromatin structure at ARS1. We examined chromatin around ARS1 in sas2Delta and orc2-1 single and double mutants using micrococcol nuclease (MNase) digestion followed by indirect end labeling. The ARS1 region is associated with three major MNase cleavage sites (designated as alpha, beta and gamma) corresponding to a nucleosome-free region defined by ORC and Abf1p bound to the ACS and B3 elements of ARS1, respectively (Figure 4; ref. 8). The pattern of MNase sensitive sites around ARS1 was not significantly altered by orc2-1 and sas2Delta single mutations or the orc2-1 sas2Delta double mutation at 23 or 30degreesC (Figure 4, compare b, c and d to a, and f, g, h to e). Therefore, Sas2p does not seem to contribute to chromatin structure at ARS1. Figure 4.The sas2Delta does not affect chromatin structure at ARS1. (A) Schematic representation of the ARS1 locus. Two AvaII sites flanking ARS1 and the position of the probe used in indirect end labeling are shown. (B) Results of chromatin mapping around ARS1 in strains 1, 2, 3 and 4 at 23degreesC (lanes a, b, c and d) or 30degreesC (e, f, g and h). Chromatin was treated with MNase at 120 or 160 U/ml, and DNA was isolated and digested with AvaII. Naked DNA sample (N) from strain 1 was also treated with MNase and digested with AvaII. DNA samples were subjected to gel electrophoresis and Southern blotting. Three major MNase cleavage sites are marked as alpha-gamma. The positions of Abf1p and ORC sites in ARS1 are indicated on the right.
###end p 41

###xml 996 1005 972 977 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 1032 1035 1004 1007 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 1040 1043 1012 1015 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 1049 1058 1021 1026 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 1063 1072 1031 1036 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sir1&#916;</italic>
###xml 1104 1107 1068 1071 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 1112 1115 1076 1079 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 1127 1129 1091 1093 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
###xml 1164 1167 1128 1131 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 1172 1175 1136 1139 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 1304 1308 1268 1272 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 1367 1369 1331 1333 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
SAS2 was originally identified as a gene involved in transcriptional silencing (13,17). However, conflicting effects of sas2Delta on silencing have been reported. The sas2Delta was found to eliminate HML silencing in a sir1Delta mutant, and abolish telomeric silencing (13,17). These results indicate that SAS2 plays a positive role in silencing at HML and telomeres. On the other hand, it was shown that sas2Delta suppressed the silencing defect of a HMR-E silencer with mutated Rap1p- and Abf1p-binding sites (HMRa-e**) (13,17). It also suppressed the silencing defect of a synthetic HMR-E silencer at the HMR locus deleted for the HMR-I silencer (HMR-SSDeltaI) (13). These results suggest that SAS2 plays a negative role in silencing at HMR. Other investigations into SAS2 function in silencing have only added more conflicting evidence to this apparent paradox. For instance, it was shown that sas2Delta decreased silencing of an ADE2 reporter inserted at HMR (18). A recent study found that sas2Delta reduced silencing at both HML and HMR, and sas2Delta and sir1Delta had a synthetic effect on both HML and HMR silencing (19). In this work, the reporters for HML and HMR silencing were yellow fluorescent protein (YFP) and cyan fluorescent protein (CFP) genes respectively, under the control of the URA3 promoter, and silencing in individual cells was examined (19).
###end p 44

###xml 1195 1208 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
Reconciling the conflicting results concerning the effect of SAS2 on silencing has been complicated by the fact that they were obtained from various silencing assays that involved different loci (e.g. HML versus HMR), silencers (e.g. intact HMR-E versus HMRa-e** or HMR-SS), reporters (e.g. HMRa versus ADE2) and/or genetic backgrounds (SIR1 versus sir1Delta). Since distinct silencers have different properties in silencing, and the function of a silencer is affected by its genomic context (15,35), we wondered whether the mode of Sas2p action on a silencer is also dependent on the intrinsic features and/or the context of the silencer. To avoid potential complications that might result from comparing data from different silencing assays, we examined the effect of sas2Delta on the silencing of the URA3 reporter by the HML-E, HMR-E and HML-I silencers in the same context of the HML-E silencer (Figure 5B, strains 21-27). Cells expressing URA3 are sensitive to the drug 5-fluoroorotic acid (FOA), so cell growth on medium containing FOA is a measure of URA3 silencing (36). Figure 5.A positive role of SAS2 in silencer function. (A) Schematics of the HML and HMR loci on chromosome III in S. cerevisiae (not drawn to scale). HML-E and -I silencers are shown as filled arrows with white letters, and HMR-E and -I are shown as open arrows with black letters. The directions of silencers are drawn pointing toward the inside of HML or HMR. The sites for Abf1p, Rap1p and ORC binding in silencers are indicated by stippled, filled and open rectangles, respectively. HMLalpha genes at HML and HMRa genes at HMR are also indicated. Tandem arrowheads indicate telomeric repeats. (B) Growth phenotypes of strains 21-33. Left, modified HML and HMR loci in strains 21-33. Cells of each strain were grown to log phase, and serial 10-fold dilutions were spotted on test plates and allowed to grow for 3 days. Two independent clones of each strain were examined. FOA, SC plus 1 mg/ml of 5-fluoroorotic acid (FOA).
###end p 45

###xml 413 415 405 407 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B19">19</xref>
###xml 480 484 472 476 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 498 503 490 495 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML-E</italic>
###xml 518 527 510 515 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 558 567 546 551 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 632 641 616 621 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sir3&#916;</italic>
###xml 650 658 630 638 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F5">Figure 5</xref>
###xml 709 718 689 694 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 777 781 753 757 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 793 801 769 777 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F5">Figure 5</xref>
###xml 821 825 797 801 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 876 885 852 857 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 891 900 863 868 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 916 920 884 888 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 934 939 902 907 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR-E</italic>
###xml 944 949 912 917 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML-I</italic>
###xml 951 959 919 927 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F5">Figure 5</xref>
###xml 1042 1046 1010 1014 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
###xml 1123 1128 1091 1096 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML-E</italic>
URA3 inserted to the left (telomere-proximal) side of HML was strongly silenced by HML-E (Figure 5B, robust growth of strain 21 on FOA). HML-I and HMR-E placed in the context of HML-E also efficiently silenced URA3 (Figure 5B, 24 and 26 on FOA). The sas2Delta decreased the growth of strain 22 on FOA medium (Figure 5B, compare 22 to 21). Since sas2Delta does not affect the function of the URA3 promoter per se (19), decreased growth of strain 22 on FOA medium demonstrates that URA3 silencing by HML-E is reduced by sas2Delta. It is noteworthy that in the sas2Delta mutant, silencing was not completely abolished as it was in the sir3Delta strain (Figure 5B, compare 22 to 23). The sizes of colonies of the sas2Delta strain 22 on FOA were uniformly smaller than those of the SAS2 strain 21 (Figure 5B), suggesting that URA3 silencing was reduced to an intermediate level by sas2Delta. The sas2Delta also decreased URA3 silencing by HMR-E and HML-I (Figure 5B, compare 25 to 24, and 27 to 26). Taken together, these results demonstrate that SAS2 plays a positive role in silencing by all three silencers in the context of HML-E.
###end p 47

We next examined whether the location of a silencer affects its response to sas2Delta. We deleted SAS2 from strains in which URA3 inserted to the right of HML is subject to silencing by inverted HML-I or HMR-E (Figure 5B, strains 28 and 30). Growth of these sas2Delta strains on FOA was reduced compared to their SAS2 counterparts (Figure 5B, compare 29 to 28, and 31 to 30). Therefore, the activities of HML-I and HMR-E in the context of HML-I were also reduced by sas2Delta. We next deleted SAS2 from strains 32 in which URA3 was inserted to the left of an inverted HMR-E silencer at HMR (Figure 5B). We found again that sas2Delta reduced URA3 silencing by HMR-E (Figure 5B, compare 33 to 32). Moreover, sas2Delta also decreased the silencing of URA3 inserted at the endogenous HMR locus with HMR-E in its natural orientation (compare 41 to 40 in Figure 7). We conclude from the above results that SAS2 is required for efficient silencing by all tested silencers in the three contexts of HML-E, HML-I and HMR-E. The HMR-E and -I silencers, the URA3 and HMRa genes are indicated. (B) Growth phenotypes of strains 40-43 on SC and FOA media at 30degreesC.

###begin title 50
###xml 10 14 10 14 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SAS2</italic>
A role of SAS2 in maintaining transcriptionally silent chromatin structure
###end title 50
###begin p 51
###xml 78 80 78 80 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B15">15</xref>
###xml 172 175 172 175 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 180 183 180 183 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HMR</italic>
###xml 276 278 276 278 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
###xml 279 281 279 281 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B38">38</xref>
###xml 411 413 411 413 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HM</italic>
###xml 477 479 477 479 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
###xml 480 482 480 482 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B38">38</xref>
###xml 29 34 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Transcriptional silencing in yeast is mediated by a special silent chromatin (15). One of the characteristics of silent chromatin is a distinct topology of its DNA. DNA at HML and HMR is more negatively supercoiled when these loci are silenced than when they are derepressed (37,38). As the topology of eukaryotic DNA reflects the density and conformation of nucleosomes, the degree of negative supercoiling of HM DNA can be used as a measure of the state of silent chromatin (37,38).
###end p 51
###begin p 52
###xml 23 32 23 28 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>

###xml 1097 1105 1077 1085 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F6">Figure 6</xref>
###xml 1162 1165 1142 1145 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 1170 1179 1150 1155 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 1236 1239 1212 1215 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 1248 1249 1224 1225 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 1245 1249 1221 1225 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sir<sup>&#8722;</sup></italic>
###xml 1262 1270 1238 1246 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F6">Figure 6</xref>
###xml 1323 1326 1299 1302 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 1378 1387 1354 1359 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 1470 1478 1442 1450 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F6">Figure 6</xref>
###xml 1546 1555 1518 1523 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 1616 1624 1584 1592 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F5">Figure 5</xref>
###xml 1627 1636 1595 1604 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Figure 6.</label>
###xml 1636 1645 1604 1609 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">sas2&#916;</italic>
###xml 1690 1693 1654 1657 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 1700 1701 1664 1665 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A</bold>
###xml 1732 1735 1696 1699 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML</italic>
###xml 1764 1769 1728 1733 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HML-E</italic>

###xml 1839 1847 1803 1811 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sub>GAL</sub>&#8211;FLP1</italic>

###xml 1636 2436 1604 2399 <p xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" textid="53"><italic>sas2&#916;</italic> reduces the negative supercoiling of silent <italic>HML</italic> DNA. (<bold>A</bold>) Top, diagram of the modified <italic>HML</italic> locus in strains 34&#8211;39. The <italic>HML-E</italic> and <italic>-I</italic> silencers and a pair of FRTs were shown. ###xml 1636 2436 1604 2399 <caption xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><p textid="53"><italic>sas2&#916;</italic> reduces the negative supercoiling of silent <italic>HML</italic> DNA. ###xml 2436 2436 2399 2399 <graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xlink:href="gkn465f6"/>
###xml 1627 2436 1595 2399 <fig xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" id="F6" position="float"><label>Figure 6.</label><caption><p textid="53"><italic>sas2&#916;</italic> reduces the negative supercoiling of silent <italic>HML</italic> DNA.
